                    if (navigator.connection.saveData != true) {

                        preloadLink.crossOrigin = "";
                        document.head.insertBefore(preloadLink, document.head.firstChild);
                    }
                }
            }

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Population sequencing enhances understanding of tea plant evolution"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2020 11:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-09-07"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Tea is an economically important plant characterized by a large genome, high heterozygosity, and high species diversity. In this study, we assemble a 3.26-Gb high-quality chromosome-scale genome for the &#8216;Longjing 43&#8217; cultivar of Camellia sinensis var. sinensis. Genomic resequencing of 139 tea accessions from around the world is used to investigate the evolution and phylogenetic relationships of tea accessions. We find that hybridization has increased the heterozygosity and wide-ranging gene flow among tea populations with the spread of tea cultivation. Population genetic and transcriptomic analyses reveal that during domestication, selection for disease resistance and flavor in C. sinensis var. sinensis populations has been stronger than that in C. sinensis var. assamica populations. This study provides resources for marker-assisted breeding of tea and sets the foundation for further research on tea genetics and evolution. Tea is an important beverage crop with a large and heterozygous genome. Here, the authors assemble the genome of the cultivar Longjing 43 and conduct a population genetics study to reveal divergent selection for disease resistance and flavor between the two variety groups."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Tea is an economically important plant characterized by a large genome, high heterozygosity, and high species diversity. In this study, we assemble a 3.26-Gb high-quality chromosome-scale genome for the ‘Longjing 43’ cultivar of <i>Camellia sinensis</i> var. <i>sinensis</i>. Genomic resequencing of 139 tea accessions from around the world is used to investigate the evolution and phylogenetic relationships of tea accessions. We find that hybridization has increased the heterozygosity and wide-ranging gene flow among tea populations with the spread of tea cultivation. Population genetic and transcriptomic analyses reveal that during domestication, selection for disease resistance and flavor in <i>C. sinensis</i> var. <i>sinensis</i> populations has been stronger than that in <i>C. sinensis</i> var. <i>assamica</i> populations. This study provides resources for marker-assisted breeding of tea and sets the foundation for further research on tea genetics and evolution.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Tea [<i>Camellia sinensis</i> (L.) O. Kuntze, 2<i>n</i> = 30] is one of the most important and traditional economic crops in many developing countries in Asia, Africa, and Latin America, and is consumed as a beverage by more than two-thirds of the world’s population<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Xia, E. H. et al. The tea tree genome provides insights into tea flavor and independent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Mol. Plant 10, 866–877 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d50579e713">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Wei, K. et al. A coupled role for CsMYB75 and CsGSTF1 in anthocyanin hyperaccumulation in purple tea. Plant J. 97, 825–840 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d50579e716">2</a></sup>. Originally, tea was used as a medicinal herb in ancient China, and it was not until the Tang dynasty (A.D. 618–907) that it gained popularity as a beverage<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Lu, H. et al. Earliest tea as evidence for one branch of the Silk Road across the Tibetan Plateau. Sci. Rep. 6, 18955 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d50579e720">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Wu, J. Review on ‘Cha Ching’ (Agriculture Press, Beijing, 1987)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d50579e723">4</a></sup>. Since then, tea planting has expanded throughout the world, being notably associated with the influence of trading along the Silk and Tea Horse Roads<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Harbowy, M. E. &amp; Balentine, D. A. Tea chemistry. Crit. Rev. Plant Sci. 16, 415–480 (1997)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d50579e727">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Hara, Y., Luo, S. J., Wickremasinghe, R. L. &amp; Yamanishi, T. Special issue on tea. Food Rev. Int. 11, 371–546 (1997)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d50579e730">6</a></sup>. Subsequent to its initial domestication, tea was further bred and cultivated to enhance certain organoleptic traits, primarily taste, and aroma, as well as biotic and abiotic stress resistance properties, including cold and disease resistance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Liang, Y. &amp; Shi, M. Advances in tea plant genetics and breeding. J. Tea Sci. 35, 103–109 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d50579e735">7</a></sup>. However, the genes underlying the traits that were gradually selected and expanded remain largely undetermined.</p><p>The majority of cultivated tea plants belong to the genus <i>Camellia</i> L., section <i>Thea</i> (L.) Dyer, in family Theaceae and are categorized as one of two main varieties: <i>C. sinensis</i> var. <i>sinensis</i> (CSS) and <i>C</i>. <i>sinensis</i> var. <i>assamica</i> (Masters) Chang (CSA). Of these, CSS is characterized by smaller leaves, cold tolerance, and a shrub or semishrub growth habit, whereas CSA has larger leaves and an arborous or a semiarborous habit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Chen, L., Yu, F.-L. &amp; Tong, Q.-Q. Discussions on phylogenetic classification and evolution of Sect. Thea. J. Tea Sci. 20, 89–94 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d50579e764">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Yang, J.-B., Yang, J., Li, H.-T., Zhao, Y. &amp; Yang, S.-X. Isolation and characterization of 15 microsatellite markers from wild tea plant (Camellia taliensis) using FIASCO method. Conserv. Genet. 10, 1621–1623 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d50579e767">9</a></sup>. Moreover, some <i>C. sinensis</i>-related species (CSR) belonging to section <i>Thea</i>, such as <i>Camellia</i>. <i>taliensis</i> (W.W. Smith) Melchior, <i>C. crassicolumna</i> Chang, <i>C. gymnogyna</i> Chang, and <i>C. tachangensis</i> F.C. Zhang, are locally consumed as tea by inhabitants in certain regions of the Indo–China Peninsula, particularly in Yunnan Province, China. Theoretically, different species are assumed to have experienced reproductive isolation; however, different tea species can readily hybridize, and it is thus difficult to accurately classify the offspring of different hybrids. Moreover, numerous morphological features are continuous, which makes it difficult to identify taxonomic groups<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Raina, S. N. et al. Genetic structure and diversity of India hybrid tea. Genet. Resour. Crop Ev. 59, 1527–1541 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d50579e793">10</a></sup>. The classification of tea has traditionally been based on morphology, the outcome of which is sometimes found to be inconsistent with the more recent classifications based on molecular characterization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhang, W., Rong, J., Wei, C., Gao, L. &amp; Chen, J. Domestication origin and spread of cultivated tea plants. Biodivers. Sci. 26, 357–372 (2018)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d50579e797">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Huang, H., Shi, C., Liu, Y., Mao, S. Y. &amp; Gao, L. Z. Thirteen Camellia chloroplast genome sequences determined by high-throughput sequencing: genome structure and phylogenetic relationships. BMC Evol. Biol. 14, 151 (2014)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d50579e797_1">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, M.-M., Meegahakumbura, M. K., Yan, L.-J., Liu, J. &amp; Gao, L.-M. Genetic involvement of Camellia taliensis in the domestication of C.sinensis var. assamica (Assimica Tea) revealed by nuclear microsatellite markers. Plant Divers. Resour. 37, 29–37 (2015)." href="#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d50579e797_2">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yao, M. Z., Ma, C. L., Qiao, T. T., Jin, J. Q. &amp; Chen, L. Diversity distribution and population structure of tea germplasms in China revealed by EST-SSR markers. Tree Genet. Genome 8, 205–220 (2012)." href="#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d50579e797_3">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Chen et al. Discrimination of wild tea germplasm resources (Camellia sp.) using RAPD markers. Agr. Sci. China 1, 1105–1110 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d50579e800">15</a></sup>. However, given that tea plant taxonomy generally lacks comprehensive genomic evidence, further analyses using population resequencing are required to optimize taxonomic assignments at the whole-genome level.</p><p>To gain a better understanding of the domestication, breeding, and classification of tea, we collected and sequenced samples of 139 tea accessions from across the world. High-quality annotated genes and chromosome-scale tea genomes are necessary for population research, and in this regard, the previously sequenced genomes of the tea cultivars Yunkang 10 (YK10, CSA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Xia, E. H. et al. The tea tree genome provides insights into tea flavor and independent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Mol. Plant 10, 866–877 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d50579e807">1</a></sup> and Shuchazao (SCZ, CSS)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Wei, C. L. et al. Draft genome sequence of Camellia sinensis var. sinensis provides insights into the evolution of the tea genome and tea quality. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 115, E4151–E4158 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d50579e811">16</a></sup> are considered important milestones in tea genetic research. However, these two genomes were not characterized at the chromosome scale, and the associated scaffold N50 values were less than 1.4 Mb, thereby precluding an evaluation of phenotypic variation and genome evolution in important intergenic regions. Moreover, annotation of the core genes (Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d50579e815">17</a></sup>, BUSCO) in the SCZ and YK10 genomes is only 80.58% and 68.58% complete, respectively, and this has tended to hamper further population selection analysis, functional genomic analysis, and molecular breeding research. Therefore, for the purposes of de novo genome assembly in the present study, we focused on the ‘Longjing 43’ (LJ43) cultivar of <i>C. sinensis</i>, which is among the most widely cultivated tea cultivars in China and is characterized by a number of desirable traits, including high cold resistance, extensive plantation adaptation, early sprouting, excellent taste, and a favorable aroma<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Yang, Y. &amp; Liang, Y. Clonal Tea Plant Cultivar Records of China (Shanghai Scientific &amp; Technical Publishers, Shanghai, 2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d50579e822">18</a></sup>.</p><p>Herein, we describe the sequencing, assembly, and analysis of a high-quality chromosome-scale tea genome, along with the directions of divergent selection in the CSS and CSA populations, and conduct phylogenetic analysis of tea accessions. However, details regarding the origin of tea and the subsequent routes of expansion remain to be clarified.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Sequencing and assembly of the LJ43 genome</h3><p>The predicted size of the LJ43 genome was approximately 3.32 Gb (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">2</a>), making it larger than the assembled YK10 (2.90–3.10 Gb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Xia, E. H. et al. The tea tree genome provides insights into tea flavor and independent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Mol. Plant 10, 866–877 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d50579e847">1</a></sup> and SCZ (~2.98 Gb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Wei, C. L. et al. Draft genome sequence of Camellia sinensis var. sinensis provides insights into the evolution of the tea genome and tea quality. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 115, E4151–E4158 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d50579e851">16</a></sup> genomes. To enhance genome assembly, 196-Gb PacBio single molecule real-time (SMRT) long reads (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">1</a>) were initially assembled using WTDBG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Ruan, J. &amp; Li, H. Fast and accurate long-read assembly with wtdbg2. Nat. Methods 17, 155–158 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d50579e859">19</a></sup> (version 1.2.8; Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">1</a>), which resulted in a 3.26-Gb assembled genome containing 37,600 contigs and covering approximately 98.19% of the whole genome. To further enhance the integrity of the assembled genome, contigs were scaffolded based on chromosome conformation capture sequencing (Hi–C) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">1</a>; Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">4</a>; Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">1</a>), and the final 3.26-Gb assembly was generated with a scaffold N50 value of 144 Mb. Of the 37,600 initially assembled contigs, 7,071 (~2.31 Gb, 70.9% of the original assembly) were subsequently anchored with orientation into 15 chromosomal linkage groups (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#Fig1">1b</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">5</a>; Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Characterization and quality of the LJ43 genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Characterization and quality of the LJ43 genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18228-8/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18228_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18228-8/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18228_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="428" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b> The landscape of the LJ43 genome. From inside to outside: LJ43 gene collinearity; long terminal repeat density (purple); single-nucleotide polymorphism density (green); gene density (red); GC content (blue). The chromosome units of all aforementioned features are 1 Mbp. <b>b</b> Genome-wide all-by-all Hi–C interaction. The resolution is 0.5 Mbp. <b>c</b> The collinearity of the genetic map and assembled genome. Source data underlying Fig. 1a and c are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To evaluate the quality of the assembled LJ43 genome, we estimated sequence accuracy at both the single-base and scaffold levels. The percentages of homogeneous single-nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) and homogeneous insertions-deletions (InDels) in the genome were 0.000224% and 0.000568%, respectively, thereby indicating a low error rate at the single-base level (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">4</a>). The accuracy of the scaffolding was evaluated based on three strategies. First, 5879 (83.14%) of 7071 connections in the Hi–C scaffolds were confirmed with at least two 10× Genomics Chromium linked reads spanning the connections. Second, 5374 (76.00%) connections were confirmed by at least two BioNano Genomics (BNG) optical molecules, among which 4484 (63.41%) overlapped with the connections confirmed by the 10× Genomics Chromium linked reads. In total, 6769 (95.73%) connections in the scaffold generated using Hi–C could be confirmed by 10× Genomics Chromium linked reads or BNG optical molecules, indicating that the scaffold was accurate. Third, the collinearity of the tea genetic map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Ma, J. Q. et al. Construction of a SSR-based genetic map and identification of QTLs for catechins content in tea plant (Camellia sinensis). PLoS ONE 9, e93131 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d50579e925">20</a></sup> with 3483 single sequence repeat (SSR) markers and the LJ43 genome had a mean coefficient of determination (<i>R</i><sup>2</sup>) of 0.93, with maximum and minimum values of 0.98 and 0.84, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#Fig1">1c</a>; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">3</a>). In summary, at both the single-base and scaffold levels, the assembly accuracy of the LJ43 genome was high.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome annotation</h3><p>For genome annotation, we annotated the repetitive sequences of the genome by combining de novo and homology-based prediction. We identified and masked 2.38 Gb (80.06%) of the LJ43 genome as repetitive sequences (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">5</a>). Among the integrated results, 60.77% (1.98 Gb) of the sequences were long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">6</a>), with LTR/Gypsy elements being the dominant class (49.85% of the whole genome, 1.63 Gb), followed by LTR/Copia elements (7.09%, 231.27 Mb). Comparisons with previously sequenced tea genomes indicated that the LTR/Gypsy and LTR/Copia repeats in the LJ43 genome were similar to those in the SCZ (Gypsy 46%, Copia 8%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Wei, C. L. et al. Draft genome sequence of Camellia sinensis var. sinensis provides insights into the evolution of the tea genome and tea quality. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 115, E4151–E4158 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d50579e953">16</a></sup> and YK10 (Gypsy 47%, Copia 8%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Xia, E. H. et al. The tea tree genome provides insights into tea flavor and independent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Mol. Plant 10, 866–877 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d50579e957">1</a></sup> genomes, whereas the LTR/Gypsy and LTR/Copia repeats in tea were expanded compared with those in kiwifruit (<i>Actinidia chinensis</i>) (13.4%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Huang, S. et al. Draft genome of the kiwifruit Actinidia chinensis. Nat. Commun. 4, 2640 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d50579e965">21</a></sup>, silver birch (<i>Betula pendula</i>) (10.8%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Salojarvi, J. et al. Genome sequencing and population genomic analyses provide insights into the adaptive landscape of silver birch. Nat. Genet. 49, 904–912 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d50579e972">22</a></sup>, and durian (<i>Durio zibethinus</i>) (29.4%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Teh, B. T. et al. The draft genome of tropical fruit durian (Durio zibethinus). Nat. Genet. 49, 1633–1641 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d50579e979">23</a></sup> but contracted compared with those in maize (<i>Zea mays</i>) (74.20%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Sun, S. L. et al. Extensive intraspecific gene order and gene structural variations between Mo17 and other maize genomes. Nat. Genet. 50, 1289–1295 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d50579e987">24</a></sup>.</p><p>LTR retrotransposons are the predominant repeat elements that tend to be poorly assembled in draft genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Ou, S. J., Chen, J. F. &amp; Jiang, N. Assessing genome assembly quality using the LTR Assembly Index (LAI). Nucleic Acids Res. 46, e126 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d50579e994">25</a></sup>, and it has been reported that the LTR assembly index (LAI), which approximates the ratio of intact LTRs to total LTRs, can be exploited to evaluate assembly continuity. Thus, we investigated the LTR composition of the LJ43 genome and compared it with the composition of the SCZ and YK10 genomes and found that the LAI of the LJ43, SCZ, and YK10 genomes was 11.78, 5.45, and 2.86, respectively, thereby indicating that a larger number of intact LTR retrotransposons had been assembled in the LJ43 genome. We subsequently used LTR-finder to detect intact LTR retrotransposons in the three tea genomes, aligned the 5ʹ and 3ʹ terminal repeats using MUSCLE (version 3.8.31), and calculated the Kimura two-parameter distance for each alignment using EMBOSS (version 6.4.0). To calculate the insertion time of each LTR, we used the equation Time = Ks/2μ (μ = 6.5E-9)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Gaut, B. S., Morton, B. R., McCaig, B. C. &amp; Clegg, M. T. Substitution rate comparisons between grasses and palms: Synonymous rate differences at the nuclear gene Adh parallel rate differences at the plastid gene rbcL. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 93, 10274–10279 (1996)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d50579e998">26</a></sup>. Unexpectedly, we found that the LTR of LJ43 had accumulated fewer point mutations and obtained calculated peaks of LTR insertion in LJ43, SCZ, and YK10 of 1, 9, and 9 million years ago (mya), respectively (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">6</a>). To further investigate this seemingly anomalous pattern, we performed NGS read error correction during genome assembly. Comparison of the genome sequences corrected by PacBio reads and NGS reads revealed that 98.19% of the 5ʹ and 3ʹ terminal IR sequences were corrected by NGS reads of no more than three bases (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">6d</a>). Moreover, error correction did not alter the Ks, which ranged from 0.013 (the peak of LJ43) to 0.117 (the peak of SCZ and YK10). Taken together, our analyses indicate that the assembled LJ43 genome is more complete than previously sequenced tea genomes, has a high LAI, and contains more recently derived LTRs, which results in contrasting estimates of LTR insertion time among LJ43, SCZ, and YK10.</p><p>To assist in gene prediction, we generated a total of 340 Gb of clean RNA-seq data from 19 samples of five tissue types (bud, leaf, flower, stem, and root) collected in each of the four seasons (with the exception of flowers during summer; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">7</a>). A total of 33,556 protein-coding genes with an RNA-seq coverage ratio greater than 50% were annotated, with an average gene size of 10,816 bp (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">2</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">7</a>) and a mean number of 5.3 exons per gene (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#Tab1">1</a>). Subsequently, we assessed LJ43 genome annotation integrity using the BUSCO database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d50579e1023">17</a></sup> and found that 1215 (88.36%) annotations were complete, compared with the 1108 (80.58%) and 943 (68.58%) complete annotations obtained for the SCZ and YK10 genomes, respectively.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Genome assembly and annotated genes of the three tea cultivars.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Using the genome annotation data, we determined the chromosomal locations of 26,561 (79.15%) annotated genes. Furthermore, we compared the protein sequences of the LJ43 genome with those of <i>Actinidia chinensis</i>, a species in order Ericales for which a high-quality reference genome sequence is available, and used MCScanX to detect synteny (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">8</a>). The results revealed that the LJ43 genome comprises 690 collinear blocks containing 18,030 genes, whereas the SCZ genome has 111 collinear blocks containing 1487 genes and that of YK10 has 54 collinear blocks containing 393 genes. Furthermore, we found that the extent of genome synteny between LJ43 and cocoa (<i>Theobroma cacao</i>) was comparable to that with <i>Actinidia chinensis</i> (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">3</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Gene family evolution</h3><p>To gain insight into the evolution of the tea genome, we grouped orthologous genes using OrthoMCL and accordingly obtained 24,350 groups of orthologous gene families among nine genomes (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">3</a>). In total, 1034 single-copy gene families were used to construct a phylogenetic tree for the tea genome, using <i>Amborella trichopoda</i> as an outgroup (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">9</a>). Gene family evolution was analyzed using CAFE, which revealed that a total of 1936 and 1510 tea gene families have undergone expansion and contraction, respectively. Gene Ontology (GO), InterPro (IPR), and Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG) enrichment analyses of the expanded genes revealed the expansion of gene families involved in disease resistance, secondary metabolism, and growth and development (<i>P</i> value &lt; 0.05, FDR &lt; 0.05; Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">8</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">13</a>; Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM5">1</a>). These families included UDP-glucuronosyl/UDP-glucosyltransferase (GO:0016758, <i>P</i> value &lt; 2.20E-16, FDR &lt; 2.40E-14), which catalyzes glucosyl transfer in flavanone metabolism and is related to catechin content; (−)-germacrene D synthase (K15803, <i>P</i> value = 8.01E-06, FDR = 0.91E-03), which catalyzes the conversion of farneyl-PP to germacrene D and is related to terpene metabolism; NB-ARC (GO:0043531, <i>P</i> value &lt; 2.20E-16, FDR &lt; 2.40E-14), Bet v I/Major latex protein (GO:0009607, <i>P</i> value = 4.49E-04, FDR = 8.64E-03), RPM1 (K13457, <i>P</i> value &lt; 2.20E-16, FDR &lt; 1.25E-13), and RPS2 (K13459, <i>P</i> value = 8.88E-08, FDR = 2.51E-05), which are related to disease resistance; and the S-locus glycoprotein domain (GO:0048544, <i>P</i> value &lt; 2.20E-16, FDR &lt; 2.40E-14), which is associated with self-incompatibility.</p><p>Furthermore, we used the “branch-site” models A and Test2 to identify the genes in the tea genome that have evolved under positive Darwinian selection using codeml in the PAML package (version 4.9d). A total of 1031 single-copy genes from the nine aforementioned genomes were scanned to identify genes under selection. After filtering (see the Methods section for details), we identified 74 genes that appeared to be under positive selection (FDR ≤ 0.05; Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM6">2</a>), some of which are involved in disease resistance, enhanced cold tolerance, and high-light tolerance. In this regard, it has previously been reported that overexpression of cationic peroxidase 3 (OCP3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="García-Andrade, J., Ramírez, V., Flors, V. &amp; Vera, P. Arabidopsis ocp3 mutant reveals a mechanism linking ABA and JA to pathogen-induced callose deposition. Plant J. 67, 783–794 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d50579e1432">27</a></sup> (Cha14g001590) and Serpin-ZX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Koh, E., Carmieli, R., Mor, A. &amp; Fluhr, R. Singlet oxygen-induced membrane disruption and serpin-protease balance in vacuolar-driven cell death. Plant Physiol. 171, 1616–1625 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d50579e1436">28</a></sup> (Cha09g003010) is involved in disease resistance, whereas that of beta-glucosidase-like SFR2 (SFR2, Cha05g001710) is involved in freezing tolerance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Fourrier, N. et al. A role for SENSITIVE TO FREEZING2 in protecting chloroplasts against freeze-induced damage in Arabidopsis. Plant J. 55, 734–745 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d50579e1440">29</a></sup>. Other identified genes included one involved in the maintenance of photosystem II under high-light conditions (MPH1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Liu, J. &amp; Last, R. L. MPH1 is a thylakoid membrane protein involved in protecting photosystem II from photodamage in land plants. Plant Signal. Behav. 10, e1076602 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d50579e1444">30</a></sup>, ChaUn21494.1) and a photosystem II 22-kDa protein (PSBS, Cha09g008070) that protects plants against photooxidative damage.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Whole-genome duplication and genomic divergence in tea</h3><p>To estimate the whole-genome duplication in tea, we selected a total of 3373, 3199, and 2992 gene families containing exactly two paralogous genes from the SCZ, LJ43, and YK10 genomes, respectively, to calculate the Ks values of the gene pairs. The results showed that the Ks peak of the three tea genomes was 0.3 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">10</a>), and the most recent duplication time was ~25 mya (Time = Ks/2μ, μ = 6.1E-9)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Lynch, M. &amp; Conery, J. S. The evolutionary fate and consequences of duplicate genes. Science 290, 1151–1155 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d50579e1459">31</a></sup>, thereby indicating that these cultivars underwent the same genome duplication event. Syntenic genes between LJ43 and SCZ and between LJ43 and YK10 were identified to calculate the Ks values of the pairs, which indicated Ks peaks of ~0.003 (~0.25 mya) for the LJ43 and SCZ pairs and ~0.045 (~3.69 mya) for the LJ43 and YK10 pairs (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">11</a>), thus indicating that the divergence times of LJ43 and SCZ were more recent than those of LJ43 and YK10.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Population genetic analysis</h3><p>Tea leaves from different species or cultivars are often processed to produce different types of teas according to their processing suitability and local consumer preferences; e.g., CSA leaves are often processed to produce black tea, whereas CSS leaves are typically processed to produce green or oolong tea. To investigate the genetic basis of these differences, we examined the genomes of 139 tea accessions collected from around the world (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#Fig2">2a</a>; Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM7">3</a>; Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">4</a>). The specimens were sequenced at an average depth of 13.67-fold per genome (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM7">3</a>). Given that the LJ43 genome is well annotated and has a high level of continuity, we selected this genome as the reference genome. We accordingly achieved an average mapping rate of 99.07%, with minimum and maximum rates of 96.95% and 99.66%, respectively (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM7">3</a>). After performing five filtering steps (described in the Methods section), we identified a total of 218.87 million SNPs among the tea populations, with a density of approximately 67 SNPs per kb (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#Fig1">1a</a>; Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">14</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">15</a>). We anticipate that this extensive whole-profile SNP dataset will be valuable for further tea genomics research and marker-assisted breeding.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Distribution and evolution of tea."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Distribution and evolution of tea.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18228-8/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18228_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18228-8/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18228_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="649" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b> The distribution of tea accessions assessed in the present study. The teas within the black oval had the highest nucleotide polymorphism. <b>b</b> Principal component analysis of the tea populations. PC1 and PC2 split the tea populations into three clusters. The <i>Camellia sinensis</i> var. <i>sinensis</i> (CSS) samples were found to cluster more tightly than the <i>C</i>. <i>sinensis</i> var. <i>assamica</i> (CSA) samples. <b>c</b> A phylogenetic tree of tea. <i>Camellia sasanqua</i> Thunb. was used as the outgroup, and the tea samples closest to the outgroup were <i>C. sinensis</i>-related species (CSR). <b>d</b> Structure of the tea populations. Green, blue, and yellow represent the CSS, CSA, and CSR populations, respectively. Yellow and orange are marked with arrows. Source data are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To further investigate the phylogenetic relationships among these accessions, we constructed a maximum likelihood-based phylogenetic tree with SNPs filtered from the total SNP dataset (see the Methods section for details), using <i>Camellia sasanqua</i> as an outgroup (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#Fig2">2c</a>). We found that all samples were clustered into one of three independent clades (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#Fig2">2c</a>; Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM8">4</a>) corresponding to the CSR, CSS, and CSA populations, which is consistent with the morphology-based classical taxonomy of CSA and CSS.</p><p>Principal component analysis (PCA) was used to investigate the relationships and differentiation among populations and consistently revealed the presence of three clusters corresponding to CSA, CSS, and CSR (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#Fig2">2b</a>). The first two principal components accounted for 13.08% of the total variance, with PC1 reflecting the variability of the CSA and CSS groups and PC2 differentiating CSR plants from CSA and CSS plants. We found that CSS showed better aggregation than CSA and CSR, whereas the juncture accessions of CSA and CSS were also close to CSR in the phylogenetic tree. At a K value of 3, CSA, CSS, and CSR could be readily distinguished (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#Fig2">2d</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">12</a>; Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">4</a>), which is consistent with the PCA results (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#Fig2">2b</a>). At a K value of 3 or 4, most new accessions collected from outside China appeared to have originated from CSA and CSS (yellow color, marked with an arrow in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#Fig2">2d</a>), indicating their high diversity.</p><p>On the basis of the phylogenetic and population structure results (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#Fig2">2c</a>; Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM8">4</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM10">6</a>), we further investigated individual- and population-level heterozygosity among the populations (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM7">3</a>). We accordingly found the heterozygosity of CSR (6.37E-3) to be significantly higher than that of CSA (6.29E-3) and CSS (5.69E-3) (both <i>P</i> values &lt; 0.05; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">13</a>). We also calculated linkage disequilibrium (LD) decay values based on the squared correlation coefficient (<i>r</i><sup>2</sup>) of pairwise SNPs in two groups, which revealed that for the CSA and CSS groups, the average <i>r</i><sup>2</sup> among SNPs decayed to ~50% of its maximum value at ~41 and 59 kb, respectively. These values thus indicate that the tea genomes have relatively long LD distances and slow LD decay (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">14</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Selective sweeps in the two major tea populations</h3><p>It is generally thought that the differences between CSS and CSA teas lie primarily in their flavor, leaf and tree types, cold tolerance, and processing suitability. Among the accessions assessed in the present study, the CSA population comprised three green tea accessions and 34 black tea accessions, whereas the CSS population contained 45 green tea accessions, 19 oolong tea accessions, and 11 black tea accessions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#Fig3">3a</a>). To determine the potential genetic bases of these differences, we used SweepFinder2 (version 1.0) to scan for selective sweep regions and selected regions with the top 1% of composite likelihood ratio (CLR) scores and the genes overlapping with the final sweep regions (≥300 bp). On the basis of this analysis, we identified a total of 1336 and 1028 genes bearing selection signatures in the CSA and CSS populations, respectively (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM11">7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM12">8</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">15</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Sweep genesets in CSA and CSS show different directions of domestication."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Sweep genesets in CSA and CSS show different directions of domestication.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18228-8/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18228_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-18228-8/MediaObjects/41467_2020_18228_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="490" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b> The tea types were used for SweepFinder2 analysis of CSS (<i>C. sinensis</i> var. <i>sinensis</i>) and CSA (<i>Camellia sinensis</i> var. <i>assamica</i>). <b>b</b> The pathway of terpene metabolism. The selective sweep genes are indicated by stars. The arrows bisected by equal symbols indicate hidden processes. <b>c</b> The expression of terpene-related genes in different tea tissues. <b>d</b> The expression of <i>NBS-ARC</i> genes in different tea tissues. Source data underlying Fig. 3c, d are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Using the data generated from GO analysis, we selected enriched genes (<i>P</i> value &lt; 0.05, FDR &lt; 0.05) from the candidate selective sweep genes of the CSA and CSS populations (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">16</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">17</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">16</a>) and accordingly found that volatile terpene metabolism genes, such as cytochrome P450s (e.g., geraniol 8-hydroxylase) and terpene synthases, including alpha-terpineol synthase (<i>ATESY</i>), (−)-germacrene D synthase (<i>TPSGD</i>), and strictosidine synthase (<i>STSY</i>), were significantly selected in the CSS population but not the CSA population (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#Fig3">3b</a>; Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">16</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">17</a>). The functionalization of core terpene molecules requires cytochrome P450s<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Pateraki, I., Heskes, A. M. &amp; Hamberger, B. Cytochromes P450 for terpene functionalisation and metabolic engineering. Adv. Biochem. Eng. Biotechnol. 148, 107–139 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d50579e1724">32</a></sup>, among which geraniol 8-hydroxylase catalyzes the conversion of geraniol (6E)-8-hydroxygeraniol (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#Fig3">3b</a>), which may affect the accumulation level of geraniol. Alpha-terpineol, a monoterpene found in tea, is generated by the ATESY-mediated catalysis of geranyl-PP, whereas TPSGD catalyzes the conversion of farneyl-PP to the sesquiterpene germacrene D. Strictosidine is the precursor of terpenoid indole alkaloids, and STSY is a key enzyme in the synthesis of these alkaloids (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#Fig3">3b</a>). Moreover, we found that 80% of the selected terpene-related genes showed relatively high expression in buds or leaves, whereas 33% of these genes showed significantly high expression in buds or leaves (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#Fig3">3c</a>; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">18</a>).</p><p>Compared with the CSA accessions, the CSS accessions were characterized by the selection of a larger number of <i>NBS-ARC</i> (nucleotide-binding site domain in apoptotic protease-activating factor-1, R proteins and <i>Caenorhabditis elegans</i> death-4 protein) genes, the <i>Arabidopsis</i> homologs of which, including <i>RPS3</i> (also known as <i>RPM1</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Mackey, D., Holt, B. F., Wiig, A. &amp; Dangl, J. L. RIN4 interacts with Pseudomonas syringae type III effector molecules and is required for RPM1-mediated resistance in Arabidopsis. Cell 108, 743–754 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d50579e1760">33</a></sup>, <i>RPS5</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Warren, R. F., Henk, A., Mowery, P., Holub, E. &amp; Innes, R. W. A mutation within the leucine-rich repeat domain of the Arabidopsis disease resistance gene RPS5 partially suppresses multiple bacterial and downy mildew resistance genes. Plant Cell 10, 1439–1452 (1998)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d50579e1766">34</a></sup>, and <i>SUMM2</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Zhang, Z. B. et al. Disruption of PAMP-induced MAP kinase cascade by a Pseudomonas syringae effector activates plant immunity mediated by the NB-LRR protein SUMM2. Cell Host Microbe 11, 253–263 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d50579e1772">35</a></sup>, have been shown to be involved in resistance to <i>Pseudomonas syringae</i> (<i>RPS</i>) (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">16</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">17</a>). The expression profiles of these genes revealed that 69% of the <i>NBS-ARC</i> genes subject to selection are highly expressed in spring, autumn, or winter, whereas 24% of these genes are significantly highly expressed in spring, autumn, or winter (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#Fig3">3d</a>; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">19</a>). However, among the 214 genes under selection in both the CSS and CSA populations, we were unable to detect enrichment of any genes related to flavor synthesis or abiotic and biotic stress resistance in the CSA population (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM11">7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM12">8</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>This study presents a chromosome-scale genome sequence of tea and resequencing data for 139 tea accessions collected from around the world. According to our analyses, these genomic resources will be valuable for future genomics research and molecular breeding of tea. The data reveal the genome-wide phylogeny of tea and the directions of divergent selection between the two main tea varieties, namely, CSS and CSA. Compared with CSA, in CSS, genes involved in flavor metabolism and cold tolerance have been subjected to stronger selection, which is consistent with the fact that tea accessions from eastern and northern China, such as green and oolong tea, have a distinct aroma and are cold tolerant. Our data also indicate that the CSR population is an ancestor of CSS and CSA. However, although these findings represent an important step in unravelling details of the origin and domestication of CSS and CSA, it remains necessary to identify the closest ancestor of tea and to examine a larger number of CSR accessions in the future. Due to the limitations of sampling in India, we cannot rule out the possibility of other evolutionary scenarios, an evaluation of which will require a more comprehensive collection of samples. Although several studies related to tea genomics have recently been published<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Xia, E. H. et al. The tea tree genome provides insights into tea flavor and independent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Mol. Plant 10, 866–877 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d50579e1814">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Wei, C. L. et al. Draft genome sequence of Camellia sinensis var. sinensis provides insights into the evolution of the tea genome and tea quality. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 115, E4151–E4158 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d50579e1817">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Xia, E. et al. The reference genome of tea plant and resequencing of 81 diverse accessions provide insights into genome evolution and adaptation of tea plants. Mol. Plant 13, 1013–1026 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d50579e1820">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Zhang, Q. J. et al. The chromosome-level reference genome of tea tree unveils recent bursts of non-autonomous LTR retrotransposons to drive genome size evolution. Mol. Plant 13, 935–938 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d50579e1823">37</a></sup>, considering the complex genome content and valuable contribution to both agriculture and health of tea, a future pangenome analysis will be essential for the tea research community.</p><p>The first important step in a genome sequencing project is to obtain a high-quality reference genome and call an SNP set with high confidence from well-mapped resequencing data. In this regard, the inherent characteristics of the tea genome, notably its large size, high heterozygosity (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">20</a>), and large number of repetitive sequences (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">6</a>), have hampered genome assembly. Although the genomes of the YK10 and SCZ tea cultivars were previously reported, they were characterized by low continuity compared with that of the major currently assembled genomes (Mb scale) at both the contig and scaffold levels. Moreover, the associated BUSCO scores indicated that only ~80% of the predicted genes could be identified in these genomes. Taking advantage of recent advances in sequencing and assembly technologies, we were able to sequence the genome of the LJ43 tea cultivar at the chromosome scale, generating an assembly characterized by a scaffold N50 value of 144 Mb, 88.36% gene completeness, and a base accuracy of 99.999%. Nevertheless, given the complex nature of the tea genome, further improvement in genome annotation will be desirable in the future. Combined with other analyses, our results showed that the quality of the LJ43 genome is higher than that of previously published tea genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Xia, E. H. et al. The tea tree genome provides insights into tea flavor and independent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Mol. Plant 10, 866–877 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d50579e1839">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Wei, C. L. et al. Draft genome sequence of Camellia sinensis var. sinensis provides insights into the evolution of the tea genome and tea quality. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 115, E4151–E4158 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d50579e1842">16</a></sup>. Furthermore, our whole-genome sequencing of 139 worldwide tea accessions generated 6272.74 Gb of short reads and 218.87 million high-confidence SNPs, and collectively, the datasets obtained in the present study provide the richest genomic resource for tea researchers compiled to date.</p><p><i>Camellia</i> is one of the most taxonomically and phylogenetically challenging plant taxa<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Huang, H., Shi, C., Liu, Y., Mao, S. Y. &amp; Gao, L. Z. Thirteen Camellia chloroplast genome sequences determined by high-throughput sequencing: genome structure and phylogenetic relationships. BMC Evol. Biol. 14, 151 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d50579e1851">12</a></sup>, as there are many disparities between traditional morphology- and whole-genome sequence-based analyses. Gene flow is notably widespread among tea accessions (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">4</a>; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">21</a>; Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM13">9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM14">10</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">16</a>), which presents challenges for determining the origin and evolution of tea. For example, <i>C. taliensis</i> (HZ122, HZ114) and <i>C. gymnogyna</i> (HZ104) were previously assigned to the CSA population. Bitter tea, a hybrid progeny of CSS and CSA teas<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Wang, X.-C., Yao, M.-Z., Ma, C.-L. &amp; Chen, L. Analysis and evaluation of biochemical components in bitter tea plant germplasms. Chin. Agr. Sci. Bull. 24, 65–69 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d50579e1877">38</a></sup>, is a transitional type of large-leaved tea with a growth habit ranging from tree-like to shrub-like and is mainly distributed in areas with mixed growth of CSS and CSA. In our phylogenetic tree, bitter teas (HZ039, HZ092, HZ080, and HSKC) were closely clustered with transitive teas in CSS and CSA, supporting bitter tea as a hybrid progeny of CSS and CSA. It is expected that further worldwide sampling and more comprehensive data analysis will help resolve the current debates concerning tea taxonomy.</p><p>Unlike annual crops or perennial self-compatible crops, tea has not experienced severe domestication bottlenecks between wild progenitors and cultivated varieties<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Zhao, D. W., Yang, J. B., Yang, S. X., Kato, K. &amp; Luo, J. P. Genetic diversity and domestication origin of tea plant Camellia taliensis (Theaceae) as revealed by microsatellite markers. BMC Plant Biol. 14, 14 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d50579e1884">39</a></sup>. Our results showing no signs of a clear bottleneck in the last 20,000 years (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">4</a>; Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">17</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">18</a>) are conceivably attributable to the widespread gene flow between different teas, which may have masked recent bottlenecks or may indicate that tea plants have not experienced strong domestication-related selection, and is also indicative of the complex history of tea evolution and domestication. During the expansion and domestication of tea, cultivated teas have been crossed with wild relatives, which contributed to the current genetic complexity of tea populations. This interbreeding is reflected by our observation that many cultivars and wild resources clustered together in the phylogenetic tree, with ancestral wild relatives appearing in the CSS cluster when a K value of 3 was used in the structural analysis (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">4</a>; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">21</a>; Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM7">3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM13">9</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM14">10</a>; Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">12</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">16</a>). Although China has the longest history of tea cultivation and the oldest written literature<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Wu, J. Review on ‘Cha Ching’ (Agriculture Press, Beijing, 1987)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d50579e1920">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Chen, C. The General History of Tea Industry (Chinese Agricultural Press, Beijing, 2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d50579e1923">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Li, W. The evolution of Bashu tea culture and the development of Chinese tea culture. Chongqing Soc. Sci. 10, 100–104 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d50579e1926">41</a></sup> to support the hypothesis that tea plants originated in this country, there is still a lack of consensus regarding the events associated with the domestication of tea. In this regard, Meegahakumbura et al. suggested that the origins of CSS and CSA in China and CSA in India can probably be traced to three independent domestication events in three separate regions across the two countries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Meegahakumbura, M. K. et al. Indications for three independent domestication events for the tea plant (Camellia sinensis (L.) O. Kuntze) and new insights into the origin of tea germplasm in China and India revealed by nuclear microsatellites. PLoS ONE 11, e0155369 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d50579e1930">42</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Meegahakumbura, M. K. et al. Domestication origin and breeding history of the tea plant (Camellia sinensis) in China and India based on nuclear microsatellites and cpDNA sequence data. Front. Plant Sci. 8, 2270 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d50579e1933">43</a></sup>. However, this statement should be interpreted with caution given the lack of available progenitor species of both CSS and CSA. Our data indicated that the CSR population is an ancestor of CSS and CSA. However, there remains a necessity to identify the closest ancestor of tea and to examine a larger number of CSR accessions in the future.</p><p>In the present study, we identified two interesting selection signatures in the CSS population, one of which was associated with genes involved in the terpene synthesis pathway. Terpene volatiles play essential roles in defining the characteristic aroma of tea, and the compositions and concentrations of these volatiles are controlled at the genetic level<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Yang, Z., Baldermann, S. &amp; Watanabe, N. Recent studies of the volatile compounds in tea. Food Res. Int. 53, 585–599 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d50579e1941">44</a></sup>. Different species or varieties of tea plants are characterized by differences in terpene profiles. Takeo et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Owuor, P. O., Takeo, T., Horita, H., Tsushida, T. &amp; Murai, T. Differentiation of clonal teas by terpene index. J. Sci. Food Agr. 40, 341–345 (2010)." href="#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d50579e1945">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Takeo, T. et al. One speculation the origin and dispersion of tea plant in China-One speculation based on the chemotaxonomy by using the content-ration of terpene-alcohols found in tea aroma composition. J. Tea Sci. 12, 81–86 (1992)." href="#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d50579e1945_1">46</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Takeo, T. Variation in amounts of linalol and geraniol produced in tea shoots by mechanical injury. Phytochemistry 20, 2149–2151 (1981)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d50579e1948">47</a></sup> found that the contents and ratios of linalool and its oxides were high in CSA, whereas the contents and ratios of geraniol and nerolidol were high in CSS. The main terpenoids determining the aroma of black tea are linalool and its oxides, whereas geraniol and nerolidol contribute to the aroma of green tea and oolong tea<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Wan, X. &amp; Xia, T. Secondary Metabolism of Tea Plant (China Science Publishing, Beijing, 2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d50579e1952">48</a></sup>. These distinctions are consistent with the findings of our population selection analysis, which revealed that the terpene metabolism genes geraniol 8-hydroxylase, <i>ATESY</i>, <i>TPSGD</i>, and <i>STSY</i> were significantly selected. In addition, our KEGG enrichment analysis of expanded gene families revealed that <i>TPSGD</i> is expanded in the LJ43 cultivar at the genomic level. Moreover, the flavor of different tea types has been influenced to a certain extent by consumer predilection and culture.
