On the basis of the processing suitability of CSA and CSS and the population selection analysis of the two populations, we can conclude that terpenoid metabolism is more closely related to the aroma of green and oolong tea than it is to that of black tea.</p><p>The second selection signature of interest identified in the present study relates to <i>NB-ARC</i> genes in the CSS population. Most of these genes are associated with resistance to ice nucleation-active (INA) bacteria. In <i>Arabidopsis</i>, <i>RPS3</i>/<i>RPM</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Mackey, D., Holt, B. F., Wiig, A. &amp; Dangl, J. L. RIN4 interacts with Pseudomonas syringae type III effector molecules and is required for RPM1-mediated resistance in Arabidopsis. Cell 108, 743–754 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d50579e1983">33</a></sup>, <i>RPS5</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Warren, R. F., Henk, A., Mowery, P., Holub, E. &amp; Innes, R. W. A mutation within the leucine-rich repeat domain of the Arabidopsis disease resistance gene RPS5 partially suppresses multiple bacterial and downy mildew resistance genes. Plant Cell 10, 1439–1452 (1998)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d50579e1990">34</a></sup>, and <i>SUMM2</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Zhang, Z. B. et al. Disruption of PAMP-induced MAP kinase cascade by a Pseudomonas syringae effector activates plant immunity mediated by the NB-LRR protein SUMM2. Cell Host Microbe 11, 253–263 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d50579e1996">35</a></sup> have been shown to confer resistance to <i>Pseudomonas syringae</i>, which is one of the most well-studied plant pathogens and can infect almost all economically important crop species. In addition, <i>P. syringae</i> is a prominent INA bacterium and has been proposed to be an essential factor contributing to frost injury in agricultural crops<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Xin, X.-F., Kvitko, B. &amp; He, S. Y. Pseudomonas syringae: what it takes to be a pathogen. Nat. Rev. Microbiol. 16, 316–328 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d50579e2006">49</a></sup>. Mutants characterized by alterations in the aforementioned genes have also been found to show sensitivity to chilling temperature compared with corresponding wild-type plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Mackey, D., Holt, B. F., Wiig, A. &amp; Dangl, J. L. RIN4 interacts with Pseudomonas syringae type III effector molecules and is required for RPM1-mediated resistance in Arabidopsis. Cell 108, 743–754 (2002)." href="#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d50579e2011">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Warren, R. F., Henk, A., Mowery, P., Holub, E. &amp; Innes, R. W. A mutation within the leucine-rich repeat domain of the Arabidopsis disease resistance gene RPS5 partially suppresses multiple bacterial and downy mildew resistance genes. Plant Cell 10, 1439–1452 (1998)." href="#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d50579e2011_1">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Zhang, Z. B. et al. Disruption of PAMP-induced MAP kinase cascade by a Pseudomonas syringae effector activates plant immunity mediated by the NB-LRR protein SUMM2. Cell Host Microbe 11, 253–263 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d50579e2014">35</a></sup>. Similarly, in wild potato (<i>Solanum bulbocastanum</i>), the <i>RGA2</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Song, J. Q. et al. Gene RB cloned from Solanum bulbocastanum confers broad spectrum resistance to potato late blight. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 100, 9128–9133 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d50579e2023">50</a></sup> and <i>R1A6</i> genes are involved in resistance to <i>Phytophthora infestans</i>, a further factor related to INA bacteria. Moreover, significant differences have been detected in the expression of <i>RPS3</i> and <i>SUMM2</i> in cold-resistant and cold-susceptible cultivars<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Wang, L. et al. Transcriptional and physiological analyses reveal the association of ROS metabolism with cold tolerance in tea plant. Environ. Exp. Bot. 160, 45–58 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d50579e2040">51</a></sup>. Taken together, the results of these studies suggest that <i>NB-ARC</i> genes might play an important role in endowing CSS cultivars with cold tolerance. Tea grown along the Yangtze River Basin and in eastern China is typically subjected to low temperatures in early spring and winter, and most CSA cultivars, which are characterized by large leaves, cannot survive in these areas. Some CSS cultivars adapted to cold environments survived during expansion and domestication in eastern and northern China, and after the separation of CSS and CSA, the direction of domestication of these two varieties is assumed to have diverged. With increasing tea consumption, humans began to select tea plants, and during domestication, selection for flavor and cold tolerance was stronger in CSS than in CSA. This is also reflected at the genomic level, as illustrated by the KEGG enrichment of expanded gene families, in which the disease resistance proteins RPS2 and RPS3 were found to be expanded in LJ43.</p><p>Although we found that 214 genes had undergone selection in both the CSS and CSA populations in the present study, we were unable to detect enrichment of any of the genes associated with flavor and resistance in the CSA population (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">16</a>). Thus, these observations indicate that selection for INA bacterial resistance and flavor during domestication has been stronger in CSS than in CSA.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Materials and sequencing</h3><p>We collected samples of 139 tea accessions from around the world (detailed information is presented in Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM7">3</a>). Among samples, 93 were collected from China, with the remaining 46 samples being obtained from the other main tea-producing countries. For the purpose of analyses, we selected <i>Camellia sasanqua</i> Thunb. as outgroup. DNA was extracted from the leaf tissues of all samples using the CTAB method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Healey, A., Furtado, A., Cooper, T. &amp; Henry, R. J. Protocol: a simple method for extracting next-generation sequencing quality genomic DNA from recalcitrant plant species. Plant Methods 10, 21 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d50579e2071">52</a></sup>. Libraries for Illumina TruSeq, 10× Genomics, and PacBio analyses were prepared according to the respective manufacturer’s instructions. Details of the sequencing procedure are presented in Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">1</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Genome assembly and annotation</h3><p>Details regarding genome size and genome assembly are presented in Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">1</a>. Assembly of the LJ43 genome was performed based on the Hi–C-Pro pipeline and full PacBio reads using WTDBG (version 1.2.8). The final Hi–C-assisted genome assembly was commissioned by Annoroad Gene Technology. Tigmint (version 1.1.2)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Jackman, S. D. et al. Tigmint: correcting assembly errors using linked reads from large molecules. BMC Bioinforma. 19, 393 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d50579e2089">53</a></sup> was used to detect errors using linked reads from 10× Genomics Chromium. The reads were initially aligned to the Hi–C scaffolds, and the extents of the large DNA molecules were inferred from alignments of the reads. For larger-scale gaps, we mapped optical maps from BioNano Genomics to the Hi–C scaffolds using the BioNano Solve 3.3 analysis pipeline. A high-density genetic linkage map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Ma, J. Q. et al. Construction of a SSR-based genetic map and identification of QTLs for catechins content in tea plant (Camellia sinensis). PLoS ONE 9, e93131 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d50579e2093">20</a></sup> was used for genomic synteny analysis. The markers were initially aligned to the Hi–C scaffolds using bwa mem (version 0.7.15), and correctly mapped alignments with a mapping quality &gt;1 were extracted (3483). Dot plots were generated, and correlations with the extracted alignments were calculated using R (version 3.4). Repeat sequences were identified using de novo and homology-based methods. Augustus and GlimmerHMM were used for ab initio gene prediction with parameters trained using unigenes. For homology-based predictions, we used the homologous proteins proposed for the genomes of <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i> subsp. <i>japonica</i>, <i>Coffea canephora</i>, <i>Theobroma cacao</i>, and <i>Vitis vinifera</i>. RNA was extracted from five tissue types (bud, leaf, flower, stem, and root) at four time points corresponding to the seasons (with the exception of flowers during summer). Three biological replicates were prepared for each sample (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">7</a>), and the transcript reads were assembled using Cufflinks (version 2.2.1). All of the predicted gene structures were integrated using EVidenceModeler (version 1.1.1). Protein-coding genes with a coding sequence length shorter than 300 nt and with stop codons were filtered (with the exception of stop codons at the end of a sequence). We then mapped RNA-seq reads to the predicted coding regions using SOAP2 and selected the predicted gene regions based on RNA-seq data (regions with &gt;50% coverage). The methods used for gene and functional annotation are described in detail in Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">2</a>. The sequences of LJ43 and <i>Actinidia chinensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Huang, S. et al. Draft genome of the kiwifruit Actinidia chinensis. Nat. Commun. 4, 2640 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d50579e2125">21</a></sup> proteins were analyzed using blastp with the parameters -evalue 1e-5 -num_alignments 5. Thereafter, syntenic blocks were identified using MCScanX with the parameters –e 1e-20. SCZ and YK10 were analyzed using the same pipeline and parameters. We also analyzed the genome synteny between <i>Theobroma cacao</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Argout, X. et al. The genome of Theobroma cacao. Nat. Genet. 43, 101–108 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d50579e2131">54</a></sup> and LJ43, SCZ, and YK10 (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">3</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Analysis of positive Darwinian selection</h3><p>A species tree was constructed as described in Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM1">3</a>, without SCZ and YK10. We identified 1031 single-copy gene families. The protein sequences of single-copy genes were aligned using ClustalW2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Larkin, M. A. et al. Clustal W and clustal X version 2.0. Bioinformatics 23, 2947–2948 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d50579e2149">55</a></sup>, and then the ClustalW2 data were transformed to nuclear format according to the alignment protein sequences using an in-house Perl script. Gblocks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Castresana, J. Selection of conserved blocks from multiple alignments for their use in phylogenetic analysis. Mol. Biol. Evol. 17, 540–552 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d50579e2153">56</a></sup> was used to cleave the nuclear alignment sequences based on the t = c parameter. “Branch-site” models A and Test2 were selected to assess positive selection using codeml of the PAML package. The significant sites were dropped if the 5-bp sequences around the site sequences were cut by Gblocks. A false discovery rate (FDR) value of ≤0.05 was used to filter the results.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">SNP calling and filtering</h3><p>Quality-controlled reads were mapped to the unmasked tea genome using bwa (version 0.7.15)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d50579e2165">57</a></sup> with the default parameters. SAMtools (version 1.4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078–2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d50579e2169">58</a></sup> was used for sorting, and Picard (v.2.17.0) was used to remove duplicates. The HaplotypeCaller of GATK (version 3.8.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078–2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d50579e2173">58</a></sup> was used to construct general variant calling files for the tea group (139 accessions) and outgroup (<i>C. sasanqua</i>, CM-1) by invoking -ERC:GVCF. gVCF files in the tea group were combined using GenotypeGVCFs in GATK to form a single-variant calling file, whereas the gVCF file for the outgroup was called using the option ‘–allSites’ to include all sites. The final single-variant calling file was merged using BCFtools (version 1.6), with only the consistent positions retained in both groups. To obtain high-quality SNPs, we initially used the GATK hard filter to filter the merged VCF data with the options (QD ≥ 2.0 &amp;&amp; FS ≤ 60.0 &amp;&amp; MQ ≥ 40.0 &amp;&amp; MQRankSum ≥ −12.5 &amp;&amp; ReadPosRankSum ≥ −8.0). Thereafter, we performed strict filtering of the SNP calls based on the following criteria: (1) sites were located at a distance of least 5 bp from a predicted insertion/deletion; (2) the consensus quality was ≥40; (3) the sites were not triallelic and did not contain InDels; (4) the depth ranged from 2.5 to 97.5% in the depth quartile; and (5) SNPs had minor allele frequencies (MAFs) ≥ 0.01.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Population genetic analyses</h3><p>We selected high-quality SNPs with a maximum of 20% missing data, and to eliminate the potential effects of physical linkage among variants, the sites were thinned such that no two sites were within the same 2000-bp region. Phylogenetic analysis was conducted with the final SNP set using IQ-TREE (version 1.6.9)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Nguyen, L. T., Schmidt, H. A., von Haeseler, A. &amp; Minh, B. Q. IQ-TREE: a fast and effective stochastic algorithm for estimating maximum-likelihood phylogenies. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 268–274 (2015)." href="#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d50579e2189">59</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hoang, D. T., Chernomor, O., von Haeseler, A., Minh, B. Q. &amp; Vinh, L. S. UFBoot2: improving the ultrafast bootstrap approximation. Mol. Biol. Evol. 35, 518–522 (2018)." href="#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d50579e2189_1">60</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Kalyaanamoorthy, S., Minh, B. Q., Wong, T. K. F., von Haeseler, A. &amp; Jermiin, L. S. ModelFinder: fast model selection for accurate phylogenetic estimates. Nat. Methods 14, 587–589 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d50579e2192">61</a></sup>. A maximum likelihood (ML)-based phylogenetic tree was constructed using the GTR + F + R5 model, with 1000 rapid bootstrap replicates conducted to determine branch confidence values. The best-fitting model was estimated using ModelFinder implemented in IQ-TREE after evaluating 286 DNA models. GTR + F + R5 was selected based on the Bayesian information criterion. The ML phylogenetic tree was constructed based on intergene region SNPs using the final SNP set and 4DTV SNPs. Principal component analysis (PCA) of the final SNP set was performed using PLINK (version 1.90), with the principal components plotted against one another using R 3.4 to visualize patterns of genetic variation. We also used the final SNP set for population structure analysis using ADMIXTURE (version 1.3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Alexander, D. H., Novembre, J. &amp; Lange, K. Fast model-based estimation of ancestry in unrelated individuals. Genome Res. 19, 1655–1664 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d50579e2196">62</a></sup>, which was run with K values (the number of assumed ancestral components) ranging from 1 to 10.</p><p>Population heterozygosity at a given locus was computed as the fraction of heterozygous individuals among all individuals in a given population. The average heterozygosity was then calculated for each 40-kb sliding window, with a step size of 20 kb. Individual heterozygosity was computed as the fraction of loci that were heterozygous in an individual. Average heterozygosity was also calculated using the same method. Windows with an average depth &lt;1 were filtered out.</p><p>To eliminate the influence of differences in sample number, eight samples of the CSR/CSA/CSS populations were randomly selected to calculate nucleotide diversity. To reduce the sampling error, we performed 20 repeat calculations for each population using VCFtools (version 0.1.16) with a window size of 50 kb and a step size of 10 kb. The data for each population are presented as boxplots created using R.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Selective sweep analysis</h3><p>TreeTime 0.5.3<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Sagulenko, P., Puller, V. &amp; Neher, R. A. TreeTime: maximum-likelihood phylodynamic analysis. Virus Evol. 4, vex042 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d50579e2214">63</a></sup> was used to infer the ancestral state based on ML using the generated evolutionary tree. Sites lacking a reconstructed ancestral state in a population were folded in the SweepFinder2 analysis. We excluded sites that were neither polymorphic nor substitutions, as recommended by the SweepFinder2 manual<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="DeGiorgio, M., Huber, C. D., Hubisz, M. J., Hellmann, I. &amp; Nielsen, R. SweepFinder2: increased sensitivity, robustness and flexibility. Bioinformatics 32, 1895–1897 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d50579e2218">64</a></sup>. To reduce the likelihood of false positives, the chromosome-wide frequency spectrum was calculated as the background for each chromosome and population. SweepFinder2 was run with a grid size of 100. The CLR scores from the SweepFinder2 results were extracted and merged into sweep regions when the neighboring score(s) exceeded a certain threshold, which was set as the top 1% of CLR scores. To obtain regions with greater continuity, we merged regions into a single region with a certain size threshold between regions, with the threshold being set to 50% of the size in the adjacent sweep regions. The final score for each sweep region was the sum of the CLR scores of the sites in the sweep region. The final sweep regions were filtered based on a minimum size of 300 bp. Genes overlapping within the sweep regions were extracted as candidate selective sweep genes. The GO-enriched (<i>P</i> value &lt; 0.05, FDR &lt; 0.05) candidate selective sweep genes were chosen, and <i>Fst, θ</i><sub><i>π</i></sub> and Tajima’s D values were calculated using VCFtools with a window size of 50,000 bp and a step size of 10,000 bp.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Gene expression</h3><p>Transcript-level expression was calculated using HISAT2, StringTie, and Ballgown with the default parameters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Pertea, M., Kim, D., Pertea, G. M., Leek, J. T. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Transcript-level expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with HISAT, StringTie and Ballgown. Nat. Protoc. 11, 1650–1667 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d50579e2239">65</a></sup>. The genes identified among the selection results were selected for expression analysis, and an expression heatmap was plotted using the heatmap package in R 3.4. The average expression of selected genes shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#Fig3">3d</a> was calculated according to season, whereas the average expression of selected genes shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#Fig3">3c</a> was calculated according to tissue. Student’s <i>t-</i>test was used to identify the significantly differentially expressed genes (<i>P</i> value &lt; 0.05).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Reporting summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#MOESM3">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>The RNA-seq, 10× Genomics, Hi–C, Illumina short reads, and PacBio raw data for the ‘Longjing 43’ cultivar of <i>Camellia sinensis</i> var. <i>sinensis</i> have been deposited in the European Bioinformatics Institute with the accession code <a href="https://www.ebi.ac.uk/ena/browser/view/PRJEB39502">PRJEB39502</a>. The raw resequencing data have been deposited in the National Center for Biotechnology Information (NCBI) Sequence Read Archive database with the accession codes <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/PRJNA646044/">PRJNA646044</a>. All raw sequence data are also available in the Genome Sequence Archive<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Wang, Y. Q. et al. GSA: Genome sequence archive. Genom. Proteom. Bioinf. 15, 14–18 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d50579e2351">66</a></sup> in the BIG Data Center<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Zhang, Z. et al. Database resources of the BIG data center in 2019. Nucleic Acids Res. 47, D8–D14 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-18228-8#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d50579e2356">67</a></sup>, Beijing Institute of Genomics (BIG), Chinese Academy of Sciences, under accession number <a href="https://bigd.big.ac.cn/bioproject/browse/PRJCA001158">PRJCA001158</a>. The assembly and annotation of the ‘Longjing43’ genome are available in BIG database [<a href="https://bigd.big.ac.cn/search/?dbId=gwh&amp;q=GWHACFB00000000">https://bigd.big.ac.cn/search/?dbId=gwh&amp;q=GWHACFB00000000</a>]. Source data are provided with this paper.</p>

    <a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" aria-label="Search for reference 3 on Google Scholar" href="http://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?&amp;title=Earliest%20tea%20as%20evidence%20for%20one%20branch%20of%20the%20Silk%20Road%20across%20the%20Tibetan%20Plateau&amp;journal=Sci.%20Rep.&amp;volume=6&amp;publication_year=2016&amp;author=Lu%2CH">

                </p></li><li class="c-article-references__item js-c-reading-companion-references-item" itemprop="citation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle"><meta itemprop="headline" content="Wu, J. Review on ‘Cha Ching’ (Agriculture Press, Beijing, 1987)." /><span class="c-article-references__counter">4.</span><p class="c-article-references__text" id="ref-CR4">Wu, J. <i>Review on ‘Cha Ching’</i> (Agriculture Press, Beijing, 1987).</p></li><li class="c-article-references__item js-c-reading-companion-references-item" itemprop="citation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle"><meta itemprop="author" content="ME. Harbowy, DA. Balentine, " /><meta itemprop="datePublished" content="1997" /><meta itemprop="headline" content="Harbowy, M. E. &amp; Balentine, D. A. Tea chemistry. Crit. Rev.
